    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="174f458f6a19400c82bd7f4af572c340">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '88b27261-c910-48f1-993f-5b4abcab487c'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d106734866" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Designing hierarchical porous structures is widely recognized as an effective strategy to improve ion transport and create active sites for disordered carbon anodes of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Herein, hierarchical porous hard carbon (hp-HC) is synthesized using zinc acetate as a templating agent. The hierarchical porous structure facilitates effective Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage with a high reversible capacity of 327 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Notably, the sodium storage behavior is capacitance-dominated, with the capacity primarily characterized by slope capacity. Additionally, the hp-HC retains 94.5 % of its capacity after 6000 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. This work provides a convenient route for manipulating pore types, which is of great significance for developing high-capacity hard carbon electrodes for enhanced sodium-ion storage.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cnma202400562-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cnma202400562-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted considerable interest in the energy storage market due to their short charging times, high energy density, low cost, and recyclability.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0001_R_d106734858e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Environ. Sci.">1</a>-<a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0003_R_d106734858e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - InfoMat">3</a></sup></span> Compared to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), sodium-ion batteries offer enhanced safety, a broader temperature tolerance, and greater energy autonomy. However, first-principles calculations reveal that as the size of alkali metal ions decreases from cesium (Cs) to sodium (Na), the ionic bonding between the ions and carbon atoms in the graphite structure progressively weakens.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0004_R_d106734858e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Phys. Chem. C.">4</a>, <a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0005_R_d106734858e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - J. Power Sources">5</a></sup></span> This weakening leads to a positive formation energy for sodium graphite intercalation compounds (Na-GIC), signifying their thermodynamic instability. Consequently, sodium ions cannot be effectively intercalated into graphite due to the weakened chemical bonding and the instability associated with the intercalation process.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0006_R_d106734858e451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - RSC Adv.">6</a>-<a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0010_R_d106734858e451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Fuels">10</a></sup></span> Therefore, investigating suitable negative electrode materials is essential for improving the overall performance of SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0011_R_d106734858e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a>-<a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0013_R_d106734858e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Hard carbon has demonstrated stability during the insertion and extraction of sodium ions, showcasing considerable potential application in SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0014_R_d106734858e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0015_R_d106734858e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Small Methods">15</a></sup></span> Templating approach is one of potential strategies for the synthesis of nanoporous carbon materials with desired morphologies and enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0016_R_d106734858e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater.">16</a></sup></span> During pyrolysis, generated metal oxides are embedded within the precursor framework and subsequently removed through acid washing. The pore size and quantity in the hard carbon can be controlled by adjusting the pyrolysis conditions. Micropores (pore diameter ≤2 nm) significantly increase the specific surface area, enhancing sodium-ion storage capacity and improving electrolyte penetration into the electrode, which promotes more efficient electrochemical reactions.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0017_R_d106734858e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano-Micro Lett.">17</a>, <a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0018_R_d106734858e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Eur. J.">18</a></sup></span> However, the small pore diameter can hinder ion diffusion, resulting in higher resistance, and excessive microporosity may reduce the packing density of active materials.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0019_R_d106734858e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0020_R_d106734858e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small Methods">20</a></sup></span> Mesopores (pore diameter 2–50 nm) and macropores can contribute to improved ionic conductivity, thereby enhancing reaction efficiency.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0007_R_d106734858e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">7</a>, <a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0016_R_d106734858e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0021_R_d106734858e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Carbon">21</a>, <a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0022_R_d106734858e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Rare Metals">22</a></sup></span> However, an overabundance of mesopores or macropores may reduce the specific surface area and compromise structural stability. Closed pores, while enhancing structural integrity by preventing pore collapse during charge-discharge cycles,<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0023_R_d106734858e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0024_R_d106734858e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. U. S. A">24</a></sup></span> can limit electrolyte permeability and reduce effective ion contact, ultimately decreasing overall electrochemical performance.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0025_R_d106734858e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">25</a>, <a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0026_R_d106734858e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a></sup></span> Therefore, optimizing the combination of these pore types is essential for maximizing battery performance.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0027_R_d106734858e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Mater. Today Energy">27</a>, <a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0028_R_d106734858e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Langmuir">28</a></sup></span> Designing hierarchical porous in hard carbon is an effective strategy to enhance sodium ion transport and provide Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0029_R_d106734858e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0030_R_d106734858e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. Eng. J.">30</a></sup></span> Although various rigid hard templates (such as Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, MgO, SiO<sub>2</sub>, etc.)<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0016_R_d106734858e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0031_R_d106734858e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">31</a>, <a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0032_R_d106734858e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Energy Chem.">32</a></sup></span> have been reported, these methods typically require solvents such as hydrofluoric acid (HF) or NaOH for template removal, which is complicated and causes environmental pollution. Therefore, developing a simple and environmentally friendly method for designing hierarchical porous in hard carbon is essential for the application of hard carbon materials in SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0033_R_d106734858e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Mater.">33</a>, <a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0034_R_d106734858e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">34</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>This study successfully established a convenient strategy for tuning the microstructure of hard carbon anodes derived from ammonium citrate. By employing cost-effective and eco-friendly zinc acetate dihydrate as a pore-forming agent, the pore structure and defect levels of hard carbon can be effectively modified by adjusting the amount of pore-forming agent and controlling the pyrolysis temperature. By comparing the differences between two samples, we reveal the structure–performance relationship between the pore types and electrochemical performance. Notably, the hp-HC is dominated by slope capacity, exhibits a capacity of 327 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. After 6000 continuous cycles at a high current density of 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity remains stable at 170 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. These findings advance not only the approach to microstructure regulation but also pave the way for designing high-performance carbon anodes for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cnma202400562-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cnma202400562-sec-0002-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p>The selected precursor is ammonium citrate, which is cost-effective. Ammonium citrate was reacted with zinc acetate dihydrate under heating and stirring at 70 °C. The mixture was then pyrolyzed at 600 °C for 2 hours. The resulting pyrolytic product was washed with 1 M hydrochloric acid solution and deionized water to obtain a porous carbon sample, named hp-HC. The comparative sample was derived directly from the pyrolysis of ammonium citrate and was referred to be HC.</p>
         
         <p>Chemicals: Zinc acetate dihydrate (Zn(CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>2</sub> ⋅ 2H<sub>2</sub>O, purity: 99 %) and hydrochloric acid (HCl, purity: AR, 36.0~38.0 %) were purchased from Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd. Ammonium citrate (C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>5</sub>O<sub>7</sub>(NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, purity: 98.5 %) was purchased from Shanghai Macklin Biochemical Technology Co., Ltd. (Shanghai, China); Formaldehyde (CH<sub>2</sub>O, purity: AR, 37 %) was purchased from Shanghai Aladdin Biochemical Technology Co., Ltd. (Shanghai, China).</p>
         
         <p>Material Characterization: The microcrystalline characteristics and defect structures of HCs were investigated using X-ray diffraction (XRD, Buker D8) and laser Raman spectroscopy (Raman, DXR). The morphology of HCs was observed using field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM, Gemini 360 and Hitachi Regulus 8100) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEOL-2100F). The pore structure was measured through N<sub>2</sub> physical adsorption/desorption (Micromeritics 3Flex), and the pore size distribution was obtained from the adsorption isotherm using the BET theoretical model. The open and closed pore structures were analyzed using small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS, Eiger2R 1 M).</p>
         
         <p>Electrochemical Characterization: The working electrodes were prepared from active materials, conductive carbon black, and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) with a mass ratio of 8 : 1 : 1. The mass loading of active materials was around 1.0 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, with a mixed electrolyte composed of 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> and 5 % fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC), diluted in ethylene carbonate (EC) and dimethyl carbonate (DMC) (EC/DMC=1 : 1 vol), and using glass fiber as a separator. All electrochemical tests were conducted in an assembled CR2032-type half-cell, which was assembled in a glove box (H<sub>2</sub>O&lt;0.1 ppm, O<sub>2</sub>&lt;0.1 ppm), and left to stand at room temperature for 12 hours after assembly. Constant current charge/discharge (GCD) tests and constant current intermittent titration techniques (GITT) were performed using the LAND testing system, while cyclic voltammetry (CV, with a voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V) was performed using the IVIUM testing system.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cnma202400562-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cnma202400562-sec-0003-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>During the pyrolysis of pristine hard carbon (HC), its microcrystalline structure and defect characteristics were analyzed using XRD and Raman spectroscopy. The XRD spectrum in Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0001">1a</a> displays two broad diffraction peaks at approximately 23° and 43°, corresponding to the (002) and (100) crystal planes of carbon materials, which are characteristic features of hard carbon. Compared with HC, the (002) diffraction peak of hp-HC shifts to a low angle, indicating an increase in interlayer spacing. Based on the Bragg's equation, the interlayer spacing of HC and hp-HC is estimated to be 0.349 and 0.378 nm, respectively. This observation implies that the coordination of zinc acetate dihydrate with ammonium citrate enhances interlayer spacing during the carbonization process. Moreover, as shown in the XRD pattern of hp-HC, the scattering phenomenon observed in the low-angle region typically indicates the presence of open and closed nanoscale pores. To further investigate this phenomenon, SAXS techniques were employed for a more comprehensive analysis, as described in the following sections.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cnma202400562-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2d163e4e-0183-445d-85f6-d126bd2e7aba/cnma202400562-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2d163e4e-0183-445d-85f6-d126bd2e7aba/cnma202400562-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8e50cce2-41f0-4717-bc47-f3d53aa6a027/cnma202400562-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2d163e4e-0183-445d-85f6-d126bd2e7aba/cnma202400562-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cnma202400562-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcnma.202400562" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) XRD patterns and (b) Raman spectra of the HC and hp-HC. FESEM and HRTEM images of the (c, e) HC and (d, f) hp-HC.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>All Raman spectra presented in Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0001">1b</a> are deconvoluted into five subpeaks. The D<sub>1</sub>, D<sub>2</sub>, D<sub>3</sub>, D<sub>4</sub>, and G bands correspond to peaks at 1350, 1620, 1500, 1200, and 1580 cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. These bands represent the vibrational modes of disordered graphite lattices, turbulent graphite layers, short-range sp<sup>3</sup> carbon, sp<sup>2</sup>-sp<sup>3</sup> hybrid structures, and ideal graphite lattices.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0035_R_d106734858e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Mater.">35</a>-<a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0037_R_d106734858e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Energy Mater.">37</a></sup></span> The area ratio of D<sub>1</sub> to G (A<sub>D1/</sub>A<sub>G</sub>) indicates the defect density of disordered carbon. The D<sub>2</sub> band signifies the turbulent graphite layer. The A<sub>D1</sub>/A<sub>G</sub> ratio increases from 1.80 for HC to 3.36 for hp-HC, confirming a higher defect density in hp-HC (Figure S1). This increase is attributed to the coordination between the precursor and zinc acetate dihydrate. FESEM, TEM and HRTEM analyses of the HC and hp-HC reveal pronounced differences in their morphology and microstructure. HC exhibits an irregular block-like structure (Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0001">1c</a>), whereas hp-HC features stacked nanospherical aggregates (Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0001">1d</a> and Figure S2), thereby enhancing the contact area between the material and the electrolyte. The hp-HC possesses much more porous compared to HC (Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0001">1e</a>, <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0001">f</a> and S3). Furthermore, the HRTEM images of hp-HC reveal a highly disordered architecture, comprising short-range ordered carbon layers interspersed with numerous nanopores and several closed pores.</p>
         
         <p>The porous structure of hp-HC is further corroborated by N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption tests, supporting the microscopic analysis findings. Comparative N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption for hp-HC and HC is illustrated in Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0002">2a</a>. The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption curve of the hp-HC exhibits a combination of type I and type IV isotherms. The sharp rise in the adsorption curve at relative pressures (p/p<sub>0</sub>) between 0 and 0.1 indicates the presence of abundant microporous structures.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0038_R_d106734858e661" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Chem. Commun.">38</a></sup></span> Additionally, the pronounced hysteresis loops observed between 0.4 and 1 are characteristic of type IV curves, signifying the presence of mesoporous structures. The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption curve of the HC exhibits a significantly lower adsorption capacity as indicated by its isotherm, highlighting its low porosity. The pore size distribution curve further elucidates that hp-HC exhibits a hierarchical structure characterized by microporous and mesoporous features. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0002">2b</a>, the pore size derived from the Barret-Joyner-Halenda (BJH) model reveals that the hp-HC is rich in micropores and possesses a significant quantity of mesopores. Figure S4 in Supporting Information delineates the pore size distribution of the micropore fraction obtained through the Horvath-Kawazoe (HK) model. The peak values correspond to pore sizes of 2.81 nm and 0.79 nm, representing the most prevalent pore dimensions within the mesopore and micropore ranges of the hp-HC, respectively. The specific surface areas of HC and hp-HC are 14.98 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> and 1639.66 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Information from N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption tests regarding specific surface area, pore volume, and average pore size of different models is summarized in Table S1. This substantial specific surface area, along with its hierarchical microporous and mesoporous architecture, provides numerous adsorption sites and significantly enhances the rapid diffusion of ions.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cnma202400562-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d2b673a7-2e28-42f5-81ca-5c7db17a4149/cnma202400562-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d2b673a7-2e28-42f5-81ca-5c7db17a4149/cnma202400562-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d7cee155-7ed4-4d85-a743-abaf88bec0ad/cnma202400562-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d2b673a7-2e28-42f5-81ca-5c7db17a4149/cnma202400562-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cnma202400562-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcnma.202400562" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Pore structure analysis of the HC and hp-HC. (a) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms. (b) Pore size distribution. (c) SAXS patterns. (d) Distance distribution function by SAXS.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>SAXS is used to characterize closed pores in the sample. The typical SAXS, as a function of the scattering vector (Q), features both a slope region and a plateau region.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0016_R_d106734858e709" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater.">16</a></sup></span> The slope region is associated with surface particles and macropores, while the plateau region indicates the presence of nanopores within the turbostratic structure, representing the closed pore structures formed by irregular stacking. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0002">2c</a>, hp-HC exhibits noticeable shoulder peaks at approximately 0.1 Å<sup>−1</sup> derived from nanopore (including open pores and closed pores) scattering, while the shoulder peak of HC is very weak, indicating that hp-HC possesses significantly more nanopores than HC. The pore size distribution graph in Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0002">2d</a> illustrates that the curve for HC follows a normal distribution, while the curve for hp-HC displays a bimodal characteristic. Additionally, the pore size distribution for HC is notably concentrated, correlating with the fractal dimension (D) derived from fitting analyses (Table S2). The fractal dimension serves as a quantitative measure of self-similarity within the pore structure.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0039_R_d106734858e720" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Energy Storage Mater.">39</a></sup></span> The fractal dimension of hp-HC is 2.7035, which is considerably greater than that of HC (D=1.2865), further corroborating that the modified material possesses a multi-level pore structure.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical Na<sup>+</sup> storage properties of HC and hp-HC electrodes are depicted in Figure S5 and Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0003">3</a>. As shown in Figure S5, the initial three CV curves at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> and GCD profiles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> indicates that the hp-HC exhibits a relatively low initial coulombic efficiency (ICE). This loss can be attributed to the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on the surface of the active material. The high abundance of micropores increases the specific surface area, which, while beneficial for enhancing capacity, also leads to a more significant irreversible capacity loss due to the larger surface area available for SEI formation. The integrated CV curves at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0003">3a</a>) clearly indicate that the hp-HC exhibits a higher specific current, particularly within the potential range of 3.0–1.2 V, suggesting its superior electrochemical activity. Increasing the concentration of defective active sites by pore-forming enhances the capacity in the high-voltage sloping region, while the crystal size of hard carbon is closely related to the plateau capacity.<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0040_R_d106734858e741" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nano Res.">40</a></sup></span> Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0003">3b</a> presents the integrated GCD profiles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> for the HC and hp-HC electrodes, where the total capacity can be divided into two distinct regions: a high-potential sloping region and a low-potential plateau region. Both electrodes primarily contribute to the capacity in the sloping region. Notably, hp-HC exhibits a significant increase in both sloping and plateau capacities compared to HC. The capacity in the sloping region is mainly attributed to the adsorption and desorption of Na<sup>+</sup> at defect and active sites. The introduction of zinc acetate has notably increased the specific surface area of hp-HC, resulting in additional surface defects and active sites, which further contribute to the improved capacity. Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0003">3c</a> compares the rate performance of HC and hp-HC, and their corresponding GCD curves at different rates are depicted in Figure S6. The rate capability of hp-HC was significantly improved, exhibiting reversible capacities of 327, 253, 195, 150, 117, 84, and 66 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at varying current densities. Upon resetting the current to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity retention rate reached an impressive 87 %. The hp-HC electrode also exhibited commendable rate capability. As shown in Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0003">3c</a>, a high reversible capacity of 117 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can still be delivered at 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, while HC displays inferior rate performance. Furthermore, cycling stability was also evaluated at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, as depicted in Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0003">3d</a>. Both HC and hp-HC exhibit capacity fluctuations during the charge/discharge cycles. This phenomenon is commonly observed in hard carbon anodes,<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0041_R_d106734858e772" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Mater.">41</a>-<a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0044_R_d106734858e772" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">44</a></sup></span> where structural rearrangements, such as the formation of more accessible pores and active sites, occur during cycling. The hp-HC retained a capacity of 167 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 6000 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to a capacity retention rate of 94.5 %. The high value indicates the effective preservation of the structural integrity of the hp-HC electrode, which can maintain its efficient Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability over a prolonged cycling period.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cnma202400562-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1fb58296-4e6f-4945-9a66-baf45fc96b68/cnma202400562-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1fb58296-4e6f-4945-9a66-baf45fc96b68/cnma202400562-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0f86d860-2313-4518-a524-b4c687478614/cnma202400562-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1fb58296-4e6f-4945-9a66-baf45fc96b68/cnma202400562-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cnma202400562-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcnma.202400562" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical Na<sup>+</sup> storage properties of the HC and hp-HC electrodes. (a) Integrated CV curves at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. (b) Integrated GCD profiles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. (c) Rate capability. (d) Long-term cycling stability at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">To further elucidate the electrochemical kinetics of the HC and hp-HC electrodes, GITT measurements and scan-rate dependent CV from 0.1 to 2.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup> were conducted (Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0004">4</a>). The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) for HC and hp-HC were investigated using GITT, and the test was conducted using a pulse current of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 30 minutes (Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0004">4a</a>). The diffusion coefficients were calculated using the following equation:
            <div class="inline-equation" id="cnma202400562-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/c6272773-92cf-49b9-b8ee-40a93f61f451/cnma202400562-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label"></span></div>
            </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cnma202400562-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cd79ceb4-1bb8-47a2-8c09-9781d9ceea46/cnma202400562-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cd79ceb4-1bb8-47a2-8c09-9781d9ceea46/cnma202400562-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d645891b-0d89-45ed-8ba1-707ecbfe98ff/cnma202400562-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cd79ceb4-1bb8-47a2-8c09-9781d9ceea46/cnma202400562-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cnma202400562-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcnma.202400562" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Kinetic analysis of the hp-HC electrode for Na<sup>+</sup> storage. (a) GITT profiles. (b) Na<sup>+</sup> apparent diffusion coefficients as a function of potential. (c) CV curves at different scan rates. (d) Log(scan rate)–log(peak current) profile. (e) Capacitive contribution to Na<sup>+</sup> storage at 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. (f) Capacitive and diffusion-controlled contribution to Na<sup>+</sup> storage at different scan rates.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Here, <i>τ</i> (s), m<sub>B</sub> (g), <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> (cm<sup>3</sup> mol<sup>−1</sup>), M<sub>B</sub> (g mol<sup>−1</sup>), and <i>S</i> (m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) represent the pulse time, mass of active electrode material, molar volume, molecular weight, and active surface area, respectively. Additionally, Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> and Δ<i>E</i><sub>t</sub> are determined through GITT. Throughout the charging process, the <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> for both materials exhibited similar magnitudes (Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0004">4b</a>). During discharge, <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> initially displayed a gradual decline, followed by an increase. The overall <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> in the plateau region was lower than that in the sloping region, primarily because Na<sup>+</sup> preferentially adsorbs onto defect active sites in the sloping region, demonstrating superior kinetic performance. Notably, hp-HC exhibited a higher <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> compared to HC, which can be attributed to its porous structure and numerous active sites that significantly enhance Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance. A slight dip in <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> for hp-HC near 0.5 V corresponds to the reduction peak observed in the CV curve (Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0004">4a</a>).</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">During the discharge/charge process, both surface capacitance and diffusion-controlled processes contribute to sodium ion storage (Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0004">4c</a>). The contribution ratios of the two mechanisms can be quantitatively determined using the power law equation:<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0045_R_d106734858e981" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">45</a></sup></span><div class="inline-equation" id="cnma202400562-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/1f28df1e-a0d7-445f-bbcd-c166809b75c4/cnma202400562-math-0002.png"></span><math></math>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label"></span></div>
            </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Here, <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are reaction-related constants, with <i>b</i> values ranging from 0.5 to 1. When <i>b</i> approaches 0.5, the reaction is dominated by diffusion, when <i>b</i> approaches 1, the reaction is dominated by pseudocapacitance. Based on this analysis, the fitting curve for the hp-HC electrode is presented in Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0004">4d</a>. In the discharge curve, the peak near 0.5 V corresponds to a b value of 0.58, indicating a diffusion-controlled process related to the deposition of Na<sup>+</sup> in the micropores and closed pores. In the charge curve, the peak near 0.5 V corresponds to a <i>b</i> value of 0.98, indicating pseudocapacitance adsorption behavior associated with open pores that provide numerous active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption. The exact percentage of capacitance contribution can be obtained using the equation:<span><sup><a href="#cnma202400562-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cnma202400562-bib-0045_R_d106734858e1033" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">45</a></sup></span><div class="inline-equation" id="cnma202400562-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/eb0709c3-8bb5-437d-8fb0-5f8d1bb167f9/cnma202400562-math-0003.png"></span><math></math>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label"></span></div>
            </div>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0004">4e</a> illustrates that the capacitive contribution to the capacity was 69 % at a sweep rate of 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Despite the absence of a plateau in the GCD curve, the CV analysis reveals a 31 % contribution from ion diffusion processes. This suggests that a certain degree of diffusion occurs during Na<sup>+</sup> ion insertion into the carbon interlayer. As shown in Figure <a href="#cnma202400562-fig-0004">4f</a>, when the scan rate increased, the diffusion contribution depressed, while the capacitive contribution increased.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cnma202400562-sec-0004">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cnma202400562-sec-0004-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the hp-HC synthesized through the coordination reaction of zinc acetate and ammonium citrate exhibits a multi-level structure characterized by both open and closed pores, which providing a substantial number of Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites. Compared to the HC, the hp-HC exhibits distinctive specific surface area, surface chemical properties, and ion diffusion rates, resulting in superior Na+ storage performance, in terms of high rate capability (327/174 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1/1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and excellent cycling stability.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
